Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
4 min
Birth and Name of Ibn Asakir
States the author's birth date, place, and clarifies his name and lineage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the precise birth details of Ibn Asakir and understand the origin of his family name 'Ibn Asakir'.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir's name does not come from an ancestor named 'Asakir'.

This lesson presents the opening biographical information about the author. It gives his exact birth date and place, then clarifies a common misconception about his family name. Understanding these details helps set the stage for his life and works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Note the two parts: birth details and name explanation.
2
Identify the birth facts
Find the date and place of birth. Commit them to memory: first of Muharram 499 AH in Damascus.
This is a foundational biographical fact.
3
Examine the name clarification
Notice that the author explicitly states that none of his ancestors were named 'Asakir'. The name is a family epithet, possibly from the maternal side.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author might include this clarification: to prevent confusion about his lineage and to assert the honor of his family name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مولده : ولد بدمشق أول المحرم من سنة تسع وتسعين وأربعمائة للهجرة .
اسمه ولقبه ثقة الدين أبو القاسم على بن الحسن بن هبة الله بن عبد الله بن الحسين بن عساکر، وليس في اجداده من اسمه عساكر ( وإنما تسمية اشتهرت عليهم فى بنيهم ولعله من قبل أمهات بعضهم على مافى ذيل الروضتين ) .
His birth: He was born in Damascus on the first of Muharram of the year 499 AH. His name and title: Thiqat al-Din Abu al-Qasim Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Hibat Allah ibn Abd Allah ibn al-Husayn ibn 'Asakir, and there is none among his ancestors named 'Asakir; rather it is a designation that became famous among them in their lineage, perhaps from some of their mothers, according to what is in Dhayl al-Rawdatayn.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage provides biographical details about the author. It first states his birth date and place: born in Damascus on the first of Muharram 499 AH. Then it gives his full name and honorary title: Thiqat al-Din Abu al-Qasim Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Hibat Allah ibn Abd Allah ibn al-Husayn ibn 'Asakir. It clarifies a common confusion: none of his ancestors were actually named 'Asakir; rather, the name became a family designation, possibly from the maternal side, as mentioned in Dhayl al-Rawdatayn.

Key Terms
ثقة الدين
أبو القاسم
عساكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People may think that Ibn Asakir's ancestor was named 'Asakir.
Correction: The text explicitly states: 'and there is none among his ancestors named Asakir'; it is a family epithet.

Checkpoint
Where and when was Ibn Asakir born?
A) In Baghdad, 499 AH
B) In Damascus, 499 AH
C) In Damascus, 599 AH
D) In Mecca, 499 AH

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir was born in Damascus in 499 AH, and his family name 'Asakir' is an epithet, not an ancestor's name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a name or a title that might be misunderstood by others. Is there an aspect of your identity that people assume incorrectly? How does Ibn Asakir's clarification inspire you to set the record straight, and what step will you take this week to address it?
?
Ibn Asakir's biographer took the time to correct a false impression about his family name. Is there a false impression about you—your beliefs, your background, or your character—that you have been reluctant to correct? What stops you, and what would it cost you to speak the truth?
?
The source notes that the family epithet 'Asakir' may have come from maternal ancestors. How often do you acknowledge and honor the contributions of the women in your family lineage, especially in shaping your identity and legacy? What is one concrete way you can celebrate that heritage today?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
6 min
Early Teachers and Studies in Damascus and Baghdad
Narrative of early teachers and travels to Baghdad and the Hijaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extensive scholarly network Ibn Asakir built in his early years, from his native Damascus to the great learning center of Baghdad, and the importance of direct hearing (sama') under multiple teachers.

Core Idea
Knowledge seeking required tireless travel and direct hearing from many teachers.

This passage opens the biographical section of Ibn Asakir's teachers and travels, showing how he began his studies in Damascus as a youth and soon expanded his quest to the greatest centers of learning in the Islamic world. The first part covers his early education in Damascus and his five-year stay in Baghdad, where he mastered fiqh, khilaf, and grammar. It also mentions brief visits to Mecca, Medina, and Kufa before returning to Damascus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once to get a sense of the names and cities. Then read the translation to follow the journey chronologically.
Notice the pattern: 'سمع' (he heard) followed by the teacher's name.
2
Identify the sequence of cities
List the cities in order: Damascus → Baghdad → Mecca → Medina → Kufa → Baghdad → Damascus.
This shows his dedication to seeking knowledge far and wide.
3
Note the teachers in each city
Pick out 3-4 teachers from each major city and observe their specializations or features mentioned (e.g., 'صاحب الفوائد العشرين' for Abu al-Qasim al-Nasib).
These names illustrate the diversity of his teachers.
4
Understand the study program in Baghdad
He studied at the Nizamiyya school, focusing on fiqh, ikhtilaf (differences of jurists), and nahw (grammar). This reflects the standard curriculum of the time.
The Nizamiyya was a prestigious madrasa known for Shafi'i fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض شيوخه ورحلاته : تفقه فى حداثته بدمشق على الفقيه أبي الحسن السلمي، وسمع فيها سنة خمس وخمسمائة باعتناء أبيه وأخيه ضياء الدين من أبى القاسم النسيب - صاحب الفوائد العشرين - وقوام بن زیاد وسبيع بن قيراط وأبي طاهر الحبال وأبى الحسن بن الموازيني وطبقتهم، بنفسه من والده محمد الاكفانى وأبى الحسن بن قبيس، وعبد الكريم وسمع ابن حمزة . ورحل إلى بغداد عام عشرين وأقام بها خمس سنين ولزم بها التفقه وسماع الدروس بالنظامية، وقرأ الخلاف والنحو، وسمع فيها أبا القاسم بن الحصين وأيا الحسين الدينورى وقراتكين بن الاسعد وأبا العز بن كادس وأبا غالب بن البناء وأبا عبد الله البارع وقاضی المارستان محمد بن عبد الباقي الأنصاري وطبقتهم وقصد مكة فسمع عبدالله بن محمد الغزال ورزین بن معاوية العبدرى . وانتقل إلى المدينة فسمع بها من أبي الفتوح عبد الخلاق بن عبدالواسع بن عبد الهادي الأنصارى الهروى. وتوجه إلى الكوفة فسمع عمر بن إبراهيم الزينبي. وعاد إلى بغداد يسمع الحديث ويقرأ الخلاف والفقه ثم رجع إلى دمشق. ورحل إلى خراسان ودخل نيسابور سنة تسع وعشرين فسمع بها أبا عبد الله الفزاري وأبـا مـحـمـد السندى، وزاهر بن طاهر الشحامي وأخاه وجيها وأبا المظفر العنزي . وسمع أبا عبد الله الفراوى وعبدالنعم بن القشيري وسعيد بن أبي الرجاء والحسين ابن عبد الملك الخلال وطبقتهما بأصبهان . ويوسف بن أيوب الهمذاني الزاهد بمرو، وتميم بن أبي سعيد الجرجاني وطبقته بهراة، وغيرهم في تبريز
Some of his teachers and travels: He studied fiqh in his youth in Damascus under the faqih Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami, and he heard (hadith) there in the year 505 AH through the care of his father and his brother Diya' al-Din from Abu al-Qasim al-Nasib - author of al-Fawa'id al-'Ishrin - and Qiwam ibn Ziyad and Subay' ibn Qayrat and Abu Tahir al-Hibbal and Abu al-Hasan ibn al-Mawazini and their class; by himself from his father Muhammad al-Akfani and Abu al-Hasan ibn Qubays, and 'Abd al-Karim, and he heard from Ibn Hamza. And he traveled to Baghdad in the year 520 AH and stayed there for five years and devoted himself to fiqh and hearing lessons at al-Nizamiyya, and he studied ikhtilaf and grammar, and he heard there from Abu al-Qasim ibn al-Husayn and Aba al-Husayn al-Dinawari and Qaratakin ibn al-As'ad and Abu al-'Izz ibn Kadis and Abu Ghalib ibn al-Banna' and Abu 'Abd Allah al-Bari' and Qadi al-Maristan Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Baqi al-Ansari and their class. And he set out for Mecca and heard from 'Abd Allah ibn Muhammad al-Ghazzal and Ruzayn ibn Mu'awiya al-'Abdari. And he moved to Medina and heard there from Abu al-Futuh 'Abd al-Khalaq ibn 'Abd al-Wasi' ibn 'Abd al-Hadi al-Ansari al-Harawi. And he headed to Kufa and heard from 'Umar ibn Ibrahim al-Zaynabi. And he returned to Baghdad hearing hadith and studying ikhtilaf and fiqh, then returned to Damascus. And he traveled to Khurasan and entered Nishapur in the year 529 AH and heard there from Abu 'Abd Allah al-Fazzari and Abu Muhammad al-Sindi, and Zahir ibn Tahir al-Shahami and his brother Wajih, and Abu al-Muzaffar al-'Anzi. And he heard from Abu 'Abd Allah al-Farawi and 'Abd al-Ni'm ibn al-Qushayri and Sa'id ibn Abi al-Raja' and al-Husayn ibn 'Abd al-Malik al-Khallal and their class in Isfahan. And Yusuf ibn Ayyub al-Hamadani al-Zahid in Merv, and Tamim ibn Abi Sa'id al-Jurjani and his class in Herat, and others in Tabriz.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of Ibn Asakir's teachers and scholarly travels. It begins with his early studies in Damascus at a young age, listing several teachers such as Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami and others, with his father and brother assisting. He then traveled to Baghdad in 520 AH, studying at the Nizamiyya school for five years, covering fiqh, khilaf (disputes), and nahw (grammar). He also went to Mecca, Medina, Kufa, returned to Baghdad, then traveled to Khurasan (Nishapur), Isfahan, Merv, Herat, and Tabriz. The passage lists many specific teachers and narrators in each city, demonstrating the breadth of his scholarly network.

Key Terms
تفقه
سمع
الخلاف
النحو
النظامية
شيوخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Asakir only studied in Damascus and relied on a single teacher.
Correction: The passage shows he studied in multiple cities (Damascus, Baghdad, Mecca, Medina, Kufa) under many teachers, demonstrating his extensive travels and diverse education.

Checkpoint
In which year did Ibn Asakir travel to Baghdad, and how long did he stay?
A) 520 AH, five years
B) 505 AH, two years
C) 529 AH, three years
D) 510 AH, four years

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir's quest for knowledge involved traveling to multiple cities and learning from numerous teachers, establishing a wide scholarly network from an early age.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Asakir traveled for years, leaving his home, to learn from many teachers. How comfortable has your own pursuit of knowledge been? Are you willing to sacrifice your comfort and convenience to seek deeper understanding of your faith? What is one specific change you can make this week to prioritize learning over ease?
?
His father and brother actively facilitated his early education. Who in your life has supported your faith or knowledge? Have you expressed genuine gratitude to them? And importantly, are you actively guiding someone else toward beneficial knowledge?
?
He heard directly from many teachers, ensuring personal connection. In an age of online lectures and podcasts, how can you ensure that your learning is not merely passive consumption but involves direct engagement with scholars and personal accountability? What step will you take to move from passive listening to active, personal learning?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner

Later Journeys to Khurasan, Isfahan, and Beyond
Narrative of later travels to Khurasan and other eastern cities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir continued his scholarly travels later in life, visiting Khurasan, Isfahan, Merv, Herat, and other cities, and the teachers he encountered there.

Core Idea
Even after years of study, Ibn Asakir journeyed eastward to learn from the great scholars of Khurasan and Persia.

This passage continues the account of Ibn Asakir's teachers and travels, focusing on his later journeys after establishing himself in Damascus. He traveled to Khurasan, entering Nishapur in 529 AH, and then visited Isfahan, Merv, Herat, and Tabriz. The passage lists the distinguished teachers he heard from in each city, further illustrating his dedication to seeking knowledge from the most eminent scholars of his time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the sequence of cities: Khurasan (Nishapur), Isfahan, Merv, Herat, Tabriz.
Notice the year 529 AH for his entry into Nishapur.
2
Identify the teachers in each city
List the teachers mentioned in Nishapur, Isfahan, Merv, Herat, and Tabriz. Note any special descriptions like 'الزاهد' (the ascetic) for Yusuf al-Hamadani.
These teachers represent different regions and specializations.
3
Compare with earlier travels
Notice that his later travels are more extensive eastward, going as far as Khurasan, which was a major center of hadith and Sufism.
This shows his determination to collect hadith from the furthest reaches of the Islamic world.

Source Text

In which year did Ibn Asakir enter Nishapur during his travels to Khurasan?
A) 529 AH
B) 520 AH

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir's later travels to the eastern Islamic world show his relentless pursuit of knowledge, collecting hadith from a wide network of scholars in Khurasan and Persia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Asakir traversed vast distances, enduring hardship to sit at the feet of scholars. What is the farthest you have gone—or are willing to go—to learn your deen? Will you let comfort and convenience prevent you from seeking knowledge that could save your soul?
?
He heard from dozens of teachers, collecting treasures of hadith. But are you even attending the local classes available to you? What excuse will you give Allah when He asks why you neglected the knowledge that was within reach?
?
Ibn Asakir's journey was a lifetime investment for the Hereafter. Are you investing your time, money, and energy in pursuits that will benefit you after death—or only in this fleeting world? Commit today to one concrete step in seeking sacred knowledge.

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
4 min
Ibn Asakir's Early Teachers and Travels
Enumerates Ibn Asakir's early teachers and his travels to major cities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the breadth of Ibn Asakir's early education, including his teachers in Damascus and his travels to major Islamic learning centers.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir traversed the Islamic world to gather hadith and fiqh from the greatest scholars of his time.

Ibn Asakir's pursuit of knowledge began in Damascus under his father's guidance, but he soon ventured far beyond. This passage traces his journey from Damascus to Baghdad, then to Mecca, Medina, and Kufa, showing his dedication to learning from a wide range of scholars. It reveals the network of traditional learning in the 12th century.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the sequence
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the places and scholars mentioned. Then read the translation to understand the details.
Notice the use of 'سمع' (heard) for hadith and 'تفقه' (studied fiqh).
2
Identify key locations and subjects
List the cities Ibn Asakir visited and what he studied in each: Damascus (fiqh, hadith), Baghdad (fiqh, khilaf, grammar), Mecca and Medina (hadith), Kufa (hadith).
His studies in Baghdad included attending the Nizamiyya school.
3
Appreciate the list of teachers
Count how many teachers are named. This shows the breadth of his network and the care with which he sought out authoritative scholars.
His father and brother played a role in his early education.
4
Reflect on his dedication
Consider what motivated a young scholar to travel so extensively. This journey laid the foundation for his later works, including Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari.
He spent five years in Baghdad alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض شيوخه ورحلاته : تفقه فى حداثته بدمشق على الفقيه أبي الحسن السلمي، وسمع فيها سنة خمس وخمسمائة باعتناء أبيه وأخيه ضياء الدين من أبى القاسم النسيب - صاحب الفوائد العشرين - وقوام بن زیاد وسبيع بن قيراط وأبي طاهر الحبال وأبى الحسن بن الموازيني وطبقتهم، بنفسه من والده محمد الاكفانى وأبى الحسن بن قبيس، وعبد الكريم وسمع ابن حمزة . ورحل إلى بغداد عام عشرين وأقام بها خمس سنين ولزم بها التفقه وسماع الدروس بالنظامية، وقرأ الخلاف والنحو، وسمع فيها أبا القاسم بن الحصين وأبا الحسين الدينورى وقراتكين بن الاسعد وأبا العز بن كادس وأبا غالب بن البناء وأبا عبد الله البارع وقاضی المارستان محمد بن عبد الباقي الأنصاري وطبقتهم وقصد مكة فسمع عبدالله بن محمد الغزال ورزین بن معاوية العبدرى . وانتقل إلى المدينة فسمع بها من أبي الفتوح عبد الخلاق بن عبدالواسع بن عبد الهادي الأنصارى الهروى. وتوجه إلى الكوفة فسمع عمر بن إبراهيم الزينبي. وعاد إلى بغداد يسمع الحديث ويقرأ الخلاف والفقه ثم رجع إلى دمشق.
Some of his shaykhs and his travels: He studied fiqh in his youth in Damascus under the faqih Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami, and he heard (hadith) there in the year 505 AH under the care of his father and his brother Diya' al-Din from Abu al-Qasim al-Nasib – author of al-Fawa'id al-'Ishrin – and Qawwam ibn Ziyad and Subay' ibn Qirat and Abu Tahir al-Habbal and Abu al-Hasan ibn al-Mawazini and their contemporaries, and by himself from his father Muhammad al-Akfani and Abu al-Hasan ibn Qubays and Abd al-Karim, and he heard Ibn Hamza. And he traveled to Baghdad in the year 520 (AH) and stayed there five years, devoting himself to the study of fiqh and attending lessons at al-Nizamiyya, and he studied khilaf and grammar, and he heard there Abu al-Qasim ibn al-Husayn and Abu al-Husayn al-Dinawari and Qaratakin ibn al-As'ad and Abu al-'Izz ibn Kadis and Abu Ghalib ibn al-Banna' and Abu Abd Allah al-Bari' and Qadi al-Maristan Muhammad ibn Abd al-Baqi al-Ansari and their contemporaries. And he headed to Mecca and heard from Abd Allah ibn Muhammad al-Ghazal and Ruzayn ibn Mu'awiya al-Abdari. And he moved to Medina and heard there from Abu al-Futuh Abd al-Khaliq ibn Abd al-Wasi' ibn Abd al-Hadi al-Ansari al-Harawi. And he went to Kufa and heard from Umar ibn Ibrahim al-Zaynabi. And he returned to Baghdad, hearing hadith and reading khilaf and fiqh, then returned to Damascus.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage describes Ibn Asakir's early teachers and his first major travels. It begins with his studies in Damascus under Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami and others, then his move to Baghdad in 520 AH (1126 CE) where he spent five years at the Nizamiyya school, studying fiqh, khilaf (disputed legal issues), and grammar. He heard hadith from many scholars there. After Baghdad, he journeyed to Mecca, where he heard from Abd Allah al-Ghazal and Ruzayn al-Abdari, then to Medina to hear from Abu al-Futuh al-Ansari, and then to Kufa to hear from Umar al-Zaynabi. He then returned to Baghdad for more study before finally coming back to Damascus. This demonstrates his dedication to seeking knowledge from multiple centers of learning.

Key Terms

السماع
النظامية
الخلاف
الحجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Asakir only traveled to Baghdad for his studies.
Correction: He also traveled to Mecca, Medina, and Kufa, and returned to Baghdad before going back to Damascus.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir's early travels laid the foundation for his vast knowledge and his monumental work, Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Asakir left his home for five years to study in Baghdad. When was the last time you left your comfort zone to seek knowledge that could transform your heart? What would you be willing to sacrifice for the truth?
?
He learned from dozens of teachers across many cities. Do you actively seek diverse, qualified scholars to deepen your understanding, or do you rely on a single source out of convenience? Whose teaching are you missing?
?
His father and brother accompanied him at first, but later he traveled alone. Do you have the courage to pursue knowledge even when no one supports you? What truth are you avoiding because it requires too much effort or loneliness?

Ibn Asakir's travels to Khurasan and list of cities visited.

Ibn Asakir's journeys reflect a relentless pursuit of hadith across the Islamic world.

Imam Ibn Asakir devoted years to traveling across the Islamic world to collect hadith from as many teachers as possible. This passage details his journey to Khurasan and surrounding regions, listing numerous cities he visited. Understanding this travel itinerary gives insight into the vast network of scholars he engaged with.

And he traveled to Khurasan and entered Nishapur in the year 529, and heard there from Abu Abd Allah al-Fazari and Abu Muhammad al-Sindi, and Zahir ibn Tahir al-Shahhami and his brother Wajih and Abu al-Muzaffar al-Anzi. And he heard from Abu Abd Allah al-Farawi and Abd al-Mun'im ibn al-Qushayri and Sa'id ibn Abi al-Raja' and al-Husayn ibn Abd al-Malik al-Khallal and their contemporaries in Isfahan. Commentary
This passage continues the account of Ibn Asakir's travels. It details his journey to Khurasan, entering Nishapur in 529 AH (1135 CE), where he heard from several scholars. He also visited Isfahan, Marw, Herat, and Tabriz. The text then lists an extensive array of cities he visited across Persia and the Jazira region, including Tus, Rayy, Hamadan, and many others. After these travels, he returned to Baghdad in 533 AH, where many people wrote from him. Finally, he returned to Damascus, spending the rest of his life teaching, dictating, and writing. The passage concludes with a remarkable statistic: he had 1,300 shaykhs and 80 female teachers, indicating his extraordinary dedication to collecting knowledge.

Commentary
This passage enumerates the many notable students who studied with Ibn Asakir and transmitted from him. This shows the wide reach of Ibn Asakir's teaching.

Misconception & Correction

Ibn Asakir's Promise and Early Praise
Initial testimonials about Ibn Asakir's excellence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir's teacher and a contemporary scholar recognized his potential and unique status.

Core Idea
A teacher's prayer and a peer's admiration foreshadowed Ibn Asakir's greatness.

This lesson presents the first two testimonials from the passage. They show the high hopes and immediate recognition Ibn Asakir received from those who knew him best. Notice the confidence expressed by his teacher, even before his major works.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
The passage begins with 'His standing in knowledge and character' – a summary of what follows. Read it and then the first testimonial from his teacher.
2
Analyze the teacher's prophecy

The word 'matter' refers to the field of hadith and religious knowledge.
3
Reflect on Sa'd al-Khayr's testimony
Sa'd al-Khayr stated he had never seen anyone like Ibn Asakir. How does this complement the first statement? It underscores that his uniqueness was clear even among his peers.

محله في العلم والأخلاق : قال له شيخه أبو الحسن بن قبيس وقد عزم على الرحلة اني لأرجو أن يحيى الله بك هذا الشأن وكان كما قال وقال سعد الخير ما رأيت فى سن ابن عساکر مثله وقال أبو المواهب بن صصرى لما دخلت همذان قال لى الحافظ أبو العلاء المقرى امام همذان يوما : أى شيء فتح له وكيف بر الناس له ؟ قلت هو بعيد عن هذا كله لم يشتغل منذ أربعين سنة إلا بالجمع والتصنيف والمطالعة والتسميع حتى في نزهته وخلواته ثم قال ما كنا نسمى أبا القاسم ببغداد إلا شعلة نار من ذكائه وتوقده وحسن إدراكه، وقال الحافظ عبد القادر ما رأيت أحفظ من ابن عساكر وقال ابن النجار أبو القاسم امام المحدثين في وقته انتهت إليه (۱). ولو رحت اذكر أشياخه الذين روى عنهم فى مصنفاته لاسيما و تبيين كذب المفترى ) الخرجت عن حد الايجاز الذي انا بسبيل منه
His standing in knowledge and character: His shaykh Abu al-Hasan ibn Qubays said to him when he resolved to travel, 'I hope that Allah will revive this matter through you,' and it was as he said. And Sa'd al-Khayr said, 'I have not seen anyone like Ibn Asakir in his age.' And Abu al-Mawahib ibn Sasarra said, 'When I entered Hamadhan, the hafiz Abu al-'Ala' al-Muqri, the imam of Hamadhan, said to me one day, "What has been opened to him, and how did the people treat him?" I said, "He is far from all this; for forty years he has been occupied only with collecting, authoring, studying, and teaching, even during his outings and seclusions." Then he said, "We used to call Abu al-Qasim in Baghdad nothing but a flame of fire for his intelligence, his bright mind, and his excellent understanding."' And the hafiz Abd al-Qadir said, 'I have not seen anyone more memorious than Ibn Asakir.' And Ibn al-Najjar said, 'Abu al-Qasim is the imam of hadith scholars in his time; the leadership ended with him.' (1) And if I were to mention all his shaykhs from whom he narrated in his works, especially in Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari, I would exceed the limit of brevity that I am following.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents a series of testimonials from notable scholars about Ibn Asakir's intellectual and moral qualities. Abu al-Mawahib ibn Sasarra recounts an anecdote wherein the hafiz Abu al-'Ala' al-Muqri asked about Ibn Asakir's methods, and upon being told that he had spent forty years solely in collection, authorship, study, and teaching, he remarked that in Baghdad they called Ibn Asakir 'a flame of fire' due to his brilliance. Ibn Asakir's forty years of relentless study earned him the nickname 'flame of fire'.

This lesson continues with a vivid anecdote that reveals how Ibn Asakir was perceived in Baghdad. Scholars described him as a 'flame of fire' due to his sharp intellect and tireless dedication. The passage also includes further praise for his memory and leadership in hadith.

His standing in knowledge and character: His shaykh Abu al-Hasan ibn Qubays said to him when he resolved to travel, 'I hope that Allah will revive this matter through you,' and it was as he said. And Sa'd al-Khayr said, 'I have not seen anyone like Ibn Asakir in his age.' And Abu al-Mawahib ibn Sasarra said, 'When I entered Hamadhan, the hafiz Abu al-'Ala' al-Muqri, the imam of Hamadhan, said to me one day, "What has been opened to him, and how did the people treat him?" I said, "He is far from all this; for forty years he has been occupied only with collecting, authoring, studying, and teaching, even during his outings and seclusions." Then he said, "We used to call Abu al-Qasim in Baghdad nothing but a flame of fire for his intelligence, his bright mind, and his excellent understanding."' And the hafiz Abd al-Qadir said, 'I have not seen anyone more memorious than Ibn Asakir.' And Ibn al-Najjar said, 'Abu al-Qasim is the imam of hadith scholars in his time; the leadership ended with him.' (1) And if I were to mention all his shaykhs from whom he narrated in his works, especially in Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari, I would exceed the limit of brevity that I am following.

Ibn Asakir's Memory and Obedience to His Mother
Biographical testimonial praising Ibn Asakir's memory and his obedience to his mother.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the testimonies of Ibn Asakir's exceptional memory, proficiency, and piety, as well as his obedience to his mother in awaiting her permission before traveling.

Core Idea
True scholars combine excellence and humility.

This passage presents two aspects of Ibn Asakir's character: his extraordinary memory and mastery of hadith, and his dutiful obedience to his mother. Through a firsthand account from a fellow scholar, we learn that he was considered the most memorizing and pious young man of his time. Yet when asked why he delayed visiting Isfahan, he humbly replied that he awaited his mother's permission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the flow from general praise to a specific anecdote.
The passage begins with a statement about leadership in memorization and ends with a personal reason for delay.
2
Identify the two main testimonies
The passage contains two testimonies: one from al-Hafiz Mu'ammar ibn al-Fakhir praising Ibn Asakir's memory and piety, and another from the same narrator about the reason for his delayed visit to Isfahan.
Notice the shift from describing his scholarly qualities to explaining his personal conduct.
3
Analyze the narrator's praise
Look at the superlatives used: 'احفظ من رأيت' (most preserving), 'أورع' (most pious), 'أتقن' (most proficient). Also note that he was a jurist, literary man, and Sunni.
This shows the high esteem in which Ibn Asakir was held.
4
Understand the anecdote about his mother
Why did Ibn Asakir delay coming to Isfahan? His answer was 'لم تأذن لي أمي' (my mother did not give me permission). Reflect on the significance of this obedience.
This demonstrates that even a great scholar's pursuit of knowledge was subject to parental permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرئاسة في الحفظ والاتقان النبل وحسن التصنيف والمعرفة التامة، وبه ختم هذا الشأن، وبخط الحافظ معمر بن الفاخر في معجمه : ثنا الحافظ أبو القاسم الدمشقى بمنى وكان احفظ من رأيت من طلبة الحديث وكان شيخنا اسماعيل بن محمد الامام يفضله على جميع من لقيناهم قدم اسبهان ونزل فى دارى، وما رأيت شابا أورع ولا أحفظ ولا اتقن منه وكان ذلك فقيها أديبا سنيا جزاه الله خيرا وكثر فى الإسلام مثله وانى سألته مع كثيرا عن تأخره عن المجيء إلى اصبهان فقال لم تأذن لي أمي.
Leadership in memorization and mastery, nobility, good authorship, and complete knowledge, and with him this matter was sealed. And in the handwriting of al-Hafiz Mu'ammar ibn al-Fakhir in his dictionary: 'Al-Hafiz Abu al-Qasim al-Dimashqi narrated to us at Mina, and he was the most preserving I have seen among students of hadith. And our shaykh Isma'il ibn Muhammad al-Imam preferred him over all whom we met. He came to Isfahan and stayed in my house. I have not seen a young man more pious, more preserving, or more proficient than him. And he was a jurist, a literary man, a Sunni. May Allah reward him with good and increase the likes of him in Islam. And I asked him many times about his delay in coming to Isfahan, and he said: 'My mother did not give me permission.'
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a biographical testimonial praising Ibn Asakir's exceptional memory, piety, and knowledge. It begins by stating that leadership in hadith preservation and mastery culminated with him. Then it quotes a written source: al-Hafiz Mu'ammar ibn al-Fakhir's dictionary, where he narrates that he saw Ibn Asakir at Mina and considered him the most memorizing among hadith students. The shaykh Isma'il ibn Muhammad al-Imam also preferred him. Ibn Asakir came to Isfahan and stayed in the narrator's house. The narrator emphasizes he never saw a young man more pious (ورع), more preserving (أحفظ), or more proficient (أتقن). He was also a jurist, a literary man, and a Sunni. The narrator asks about his delay in coming to Isfahan, and Ibn Asakir replies that his mother did not give him permission. This illustrates his obedience to his mother and humility.

إتقان
ورع
سني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Asakir delayed coming to Isfahan because he was busy or unwilling.
Correction: The passage explicitly states he awaited his mother's permission, showing his obedience.

Checkpoint

B) His mother did not allow him
C) He had no desire
D) He was ill

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir was praised for his unmatched memory and piety, and his humility is shown by his awaiting his mother's permission before traveling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you delayed a goal or opportunity out of obedience to a parent, and how did that feel? Are there current plans where you are ignoring a parent's unspoken wish?
?
Your pursuit of excellence: are you as concerned with your piety and obedience to parents as you are with memorizing or mastering your field? What would Ibn Asakir's example cost you today?
?
If you were asked why you delayed a good deed, would your answer be an honorable one like 'my mother did not permit me', or would it reveal a lack of submission? What does your answer say about your heart?

Ibn Asakir's Peerless Memory and Status
Two anecdotes demonstrating Ibn Asakir's unmatched memory and scholarly status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two anecdotes from al-Hamadhani and Ibn al-Sam'ani illustrate Imam Ibn Asakir's extraordinary status as a hafiz and his immense memory.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir's memory was so remarkable that even the greatest scholars praised him.

This passage presents two compelling stories that highlight the exceptional status of Imam Ibn Asakir. The first shows al-Hamadhani making an unprecedented exception for traveling to Ibn Asakir, while the second demonstrates Ibn Asakir's incredible ability to recite a book from memory. Together, they paint a vivid picture of why he was considered a peerless hafiz.

Guided Path
1
Read the two anecdotes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two distinct stories: al-Hamadhani's permission and the memory incident.
2
Analyze the first anecdote
What does al-Hamadhani's exception tell us about Ibn Asakir's rank? He permits travel only to someone 'equal or better,' but makes an exception for Ibn Asakir, calling him 'a hafiz as required'.
3
Reflect on the memory story
Ibn Asakir recited the book 'al-Ba'th wa al-Nushur' from memory to comfort Ibn al-Sam'ani. Consider what this reveals about his memory and generosity.
The doubt about whether he recited all or part does not diminish the feat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الحافظ أبو العلاء الهمذاني لبعض تلامذته وقد استأذنه أن يسافر ان عرفت استاذا اعلم منى أو يكون فى الفضل مثلى فحينئذ آذن لك ان تسافر إليه اللهم إلا أن تسافر إلى الشيخ الحافظ ابن عساكر فإنه حافظ كما يجب، قال . وسمعت شيخنا عبدالوهاب بن الأمين قال : كنت يوما مع الحافظ أبي القاسم بن عساكر وأبى سعد بن السمعاني نمشي في طلب الحديث ولقاء الشيوخ فلقينا شيخا فاستوقفه ابن السمعانى ليقرأ عليه شيئا وطاف على الجزء الذ هو سماعه في خريطته فلم يجده وضاق صدره فقال له ابن عساكر ما الجزء الذي هو سماعه قال كتاب البعث والنشور لابن أبي داود سمعه من أبى نصر الزينبي فقال له : لا تحزن وقرأه عليه من حفظه أو بعضه قال ابن النجار : الشك من شيخنا.
Al-Hafiz Abu al-'Ala' al-Hamadhani said to one of his students who asked his permission to travel: 'If you know a teacher more learned than me or equal to me in virtue, then I will permit you to travel to him. Except that you travel to the shaykh al-Hafiz Ibn 'Asakir, for he is a hafiz as is required.' He said: 'And I heard our shaykh 'Abd al-Wahhab ibn al-Amin say: I was one day with al-Hafiz Abu al-Qasim ibn 'Asakir and Abu Sa'd ibn al-Sam'ani walking in pursuit of hadith and meeting shaykhs. We met an old man, and Ibn al-Sam'ani stopped him to read something to him. He searched for the part (juz') that he had listening permission for (sama') in his bag, but could not find it, and became distressed. Ibn 'Asakir said to him: What is the part that you have sama' for? He said: The book of al-Ba'th wa al-Nushur by Ibn Abi Dawud, which he heard from Abu Nasr al-Zaynabi. He said to him: Do not grieve, and he recited it to him from memory, or part of it. Ibn al-Najjar said: The doubt is from our shaykh.'
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage contains two anecdotes demonstrating Ibn Asakir's high status as a hafiz. First, al-Hamadhani tells a student that he may only travel to a teacher if that teacher is more learned or equal to him, but he makes an exception for Ibn Asakir, whom he considers a hafiz as is required. This indicates that al-Hamadhani considered Ibn Asakir peerless. Second, the story with Ibn al-Sam'ani: While walking, they met a shaykh. Ibn al-Sam'ani intended to read something but lost his copy of the juz'. Ibn Asakir asked him what it was, and when told it was the book 'al-Ba'th wa al-Nushur' by Ibn Abi Dawud, he comforted him and recited it from memory. This shows his extraordinary memory. The comment by Ibn al-Najjar about doubt refers to whether he recited the whole book or part.

Key Terms
حفظ
جزء
سماع
خريطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'doubt' mentioned by Ibn al-Najjar means that Ibn Asakir's memory was questioned.
Correction: The doubt refers only to whether he recited the entire book or just part of it; his ability to recite from memory at all is undisputed and shows his exceptional hafiz status.

Checkpoint

B) He recited it from memory
C) He gave him another copy
D) He postponed the reading

Key Takeaway
Two anecdotes prove Ibn Asakir's peerless status as a hafiz with an unmatched memory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How much of the Quran and hadith have you committed to memory? If a scholar like Ibn 'Asakir can recite an entire book from memory to comfort a distressed colleague, what excuse do you have for not even memorizing a few verses a week? What specific step will you take today to begin or increase your memorization?
?
Al-Hamadhani made an exception for Ibn 'Asakir, allowing travel only to a teacher equal or greater than himself. Do you examine the qualifications of those you take knowledge from, or do you follow anyone? How can you ensure you are learning from reliable, knowledgeable teachers, and what will you do if you realize your current sources are weak?
?
Ibn 'Asakir used his memory to relieve someone's distress. When was the last time your knowledge of Islam helped someone in difficulty? How can you actively look for opportunities to use what you learn in this lesson to serve others, starting this week?

Ibn Asakir's Superiority Among Hafiz
Comparison of Ibn Asakir with other hafiz, with opinions on his superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical testimonies that establish Ibn Asakir's unmatched memory among his contemporaries.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir was hailed as the unparalleled hafiz of his era.

This passage presents direct scholarly evaluations of Ibn Asakir's memory. Al-Nawawi's bold praise and al-Mundhiri's comparative questioning reveal a consensus on his superiority. As you read, notice how reverence for teachers could sometimes mask a scholar's true rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Pay attention to the names and titles used.
2
Identify the scholars and testimonies
List the scholars: al-Nawawi, al-Mundhiri, al-Maqdisi, al-Dhahabi. Note what each said about Ibn Asakir.
Al-Mundhiri's account includes four comparisons.
3
Understand al-Maqdisi's response about al-Silafi
Al-Maqdisi calls al-Silafi 'our teacher' instead of ranking him. Recognize this as reverence, not a judgment.
The commentary explains this deference.
4
Reflect on scholarly evaluation
Consider how multiple testimonies build a picture of Ibn Asakir's excellence.
Notice the consensus despite varying wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال فيه الشيخ محيى الدين النووى هو حافظ الشام بل هو الدنيا الامام مطلقا الثقة الثبث قال الحافظ المنذري : سألت شيخنا الحافظ أبا الحسن على بن المفضل المقدسي فقلت له أربعة من الحفاظ تعاصروا أيهم احفظ قال من هم؟ : قلت ابن عساکر وابن ناصر قال ابن عساکر احفظ قلت أبو العلاء وابن عساکر قال ابن عساكر أحفظ قلت أبو طاهر السلفى وابن عساکر؟ فقال السلفي استاذنا ، قال الحافظ زكى الدين وغيره من الحفاظ الأثبات كالذهبي وأبي العباس بن المظفر: هذا دليل على أن عنده ابن عساكر احفظ إلا أنه وقر شيخه ان يصرح بأن ابن عساكر احفظ منه قال الذهبي وإلا فابن عساکر احفظ منه قال وما أرى ابن عساکر رأی مثل نفسه.
Shaykh Muhyi al-Din al-Nawawi said about him: 'He is the hafiz of Syria, rather of the world, the imam absolutely, the trustworthy, the proven.' Al-Hafiz al-Mundhiri said: I asked our shaykh al-Hafiz Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn al-Mufaddal al-Maqdisi, and said to him: Four hafiz were contemporaries, which of them is more preserving? He said: Who are they? I said: Ibn 'Asakir and Ibn Nasir. He said: Ibn 'Asakir is more preserving. I said: Abu al-'Ala' and Ibn 'Asakir? He said: Ibn 'Asakir is more preserving. I said: Abu Tahir al-Silafi and Ibn 'Asakir? He said: al-Silafi is our teacher. Al-Hafiz Zaki al-Din and other reliable hafiz like al-Dhahabi and Abu al-'Abbas ibn al-Muzaffar said: This is evidence that according to them Ibn 'Asakir is more preserving, except that he revered his shaykh so would not declare that Ibn 'Asakir is more preserving than him. Al-Dhahabi said: Otherwise, Ibn 'Asakir is more preserving than him. He said: I do not think Ibn 'Asakir saw the like of himself.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents a series of testimonies establishing Ibn Asakir's superior memory among his peers. Al-Nawawi calls him the hafiz of Syria, nay of the whole world, and an absolute imam, trustworthy and confirmed. Then al-Mundhiri recounts a comparison: he asked al-Maqdisi to rank four hafiz: Ibn Asakir, Ibn Nasir, Abu al-'Ala', and Abu Tahir al-Silafi. Al-Maqdisi consistently placed Ibn Asakir as more preserving, except when asked about al-Silafi, whom he called 'our teacher' (esteemed). The passage explains that al-Maqdisi's deference to al-Silafi was due to reverence, not because he thought al-Silafi was better. Al-Dhahabi confirms that Ibn Asakir was more preserving, and adds that Ibn Asakir never saw his equal.

Key Terms
حافظ
إمام
ثقة
ثبت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Maqdisi considered al-Silafi superior to Ibn Asakir because he called him 'our teacher'.
Correction: Al-Maqdisi's phrase 'al-Silafi is our teacher' was a sign of respect, not a judgment on memorization. The commentators clarify that al-Maqdisi still held Ibn Asakir as more preserving.

Checkpoint
How did al-Maqdisi compare Ibn Asakir to al-Silafi?
A) He said al-Silafi was better
B) He said Ibn Asakir was better
C) He said al-Silafi is our teacher
D) He refused to answer

Key Takeaway
Historical testimonies confirm Ibn Asakir's unmatched memory, even when compared to his esteemed contemporaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars acknowledged Ibn Asakir's superiority despite their own achievements. Is there someone in your life whose knowledge or skill you recognize as greater than yours, but your ego prevents you from fully acknowledging it? What would it cost you to openly honor that person as al-Maqdisi honored his teacher?
?
Al-Maqdisi's reverence for his teacher kept him from comparing. Who are the teachers (past or present) that you owe a debt of gratitude? What specific action will you take this week to show them respect, not just in words but in deed?

Ibn Asakir's Prestige: Dar al-Hadith and Baghdad's Praise
Nur al-Din built a Dar al-Hadith for Ibn Asakir; Baghdadis praised him as unique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the political support of Nur al-Din and the recognition of Baghdad's scholars elevated Ibn Asakir's status as a hadith scholar.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir's legacy was built on royal patronage and scholarly acclaim.

This passage from Ibn 'Asakir's biography highlights two key moments that cemented his reputation: the construction of a dedicated hadith school by Sultan Nur al-Din, and the extraordinary praise he received from the scholars of Baghdad. As you read, notice how the Baghdadis measure him against other notable figures, placing him at the pinnacle.

وكان الملك العادل محمود بن زنکی نور الدين قد بنى له دار الحديث النورية فدرس بها إلى حين وفاته ولما قدم إلى بغداد أعجب به البغداديون وقالوا قدم علينا من دمشق ثلاثة ما رأينا مثلهم : الشيخ يوسف الدمشقى والصائن أبو الحسين هبة الله بن الحسن وأخوه أبو القسم، وقال القاسم حدثني أبي رحمه الله قال كنت يوما اقرأ على شيخنا أبي الفتح المختار بن عبد الحميد وهو يتحدث مع جماعة بالعجمية فقال قدم علينا الوزير أبو على فقلنا ما رأينا مثله ثم قدم علينا أبو سعد بن السمعاني فقلنا ما رأينا مثله حتى قدم علينا هذا فلم نر مثله.
The just king Mahmud ibn Zanki Nur al-Din had built for him the Dar al-Hadith al-Nuriyya, and he taught in it until his death. When he came to Baghdad, the Baghdadis admired him and said: 'Three came to us from Damascus, we have not seen their likes: Shaykh Yusuf al-Dimashqi, and al-Sa'in Abu al-Husayn Hibat Allah ibn al-Hasan, and his brother Abu al-Qasim.' He said: 'The vizier Abu 'Ali came to us, and we said we have not seen his like. Then Abu Sa'd ibn al-Sam'ani came to us, and we said we have not seen his like. Until this one came to us, and we said we have not seen his like.'
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage notes that Nur al-Din built a dar al-hadith for Ibn Asakir, where he taught until his death. When he visited Baghdad, the people were impressed and listed three Damascenes as incomparable: Yusuf al-Dimashqi, Hibat Allah (al-Sa'in), and his brother (likely Abu al-Qasim, i.e., Ibn Asakir himself). Then a story: a shaykh in Baghdad, while conversing in Persian, lists the three most impressive visitors: the vizier Abu 'Ali, then Ibn al-Sam'ani, and finally 'this one' (Ibn Asakir), declaring that each was unprecedented, implying Ibn Asakir was the best.

Statements of Ibn Qadi Shuhba and Abu Shama on Ibn Asakir's Rank
Ibn Qadi Shuhba calls him pride of Shafi'is; Abu Shama praises the Banu Asakir house.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the high regard in which contemporaries held Ibn Asakir, as witnessed by two scholars' testimonies.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir was hailed as the leading hadith scholar of his time by his peers.

This passage presents two glowing testimonies from prominent scholars about the stature of Ibn Asakir and his family. Ibn Qadi Shuhba calls him the pride of the Shafi'is and the imam of hadith; Abu Shama praises the Banu Asakir house as a noble family combining religious and worldly leadership.

وقال ابن قاضي شهبة : فخر الشافعية وإمام أهل الحديث في زمانهم وحامل لوائهم . وقال أبو شامة في ذيل الروضتين عند ترجمة الفخر بن عساكر وهذا البيت (بنو عساكر بيت جليل من الدمشقيين كثير الفضلاء والحفاظ والأمناء جمع هذا البيت رياسة الدين والدنيا، وأجلهم في زمانه دينا وعلما هذا فخر الدين ابن عساكر وفي القرن الذى قبله عماه الصائن هبة الله والحافظ أبو القاسم .
Ibn Qadi Shuhba said: 'The pride of the Shafi'is, the imam of the people of hadith in their time, and the bearer of their banner.' And Abu Shama said in his supplement to al-Rawdatayn, in the biography of Fakhr al-Din ibn 'Asakir: 'This family, the Banu 'Asakir, is a noble house from the Damascenes, full of virtuous people, hafiz, and trustworthy. The greatest among them in his time in religion and knowledge is Fakhr al-Din ibn 'Asakir, and in the century before him were his two uncles al-Sa'in Hibat Allah and al-Hafiz Abu al-Qasim.'
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
Two scholars praise Ibn Asakir. Ibn Qadi Shuhba calls him the pride of the Shafi'i school, the imam of hadith scholars of his time, and the bearer of their banner (i.e., leader). Abu Shama describes the Banu Asakir family as noble, producing many virtuous hafiz and trustworthy scholars, combining religious and worldly leadership. He singles out Fakhr al-Din (Ibn Asakir) as the greatest in his time, and mentions his two uncles from the previous generation: al-Sa'in Hibat Allah and al-Hafiz Abu al-Qasim.

Correction: Abu Shama says 'in the century before him were his two uncles' (عماه), meaning his father's brothers (paternal uncles), not his own brothers.

Ibn Asakir's Piety and Asceticism
Description of Ibn Asakir's religious practices and detachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the profound piety and ascetic practices of Imam Ibn Asakir, including his devotion to worship, self-accountability, and detachment from worldly positions.

Core Idea
True piety combines personal devotion, fearless truth-speaking, and complete detachment from the world.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's biography paints a vivid picture of his daily religious life. It shows how he combined regular worship, self-discipline, and courage in defending the truth. As you read, notice how each practice reinforces his detachment from worldly concerns.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage lists many practices in a flowing style.
2
Identify the key practices
List the specific acts of worship mentioned: regular congregational prayer, completing a khatma every Friday and daily in Ramadan, i'tikaf, supererogatory prayers, and adhkar.
Note the frequency: weekly, daily, special occasions.
3
Reflect on self-accountability
Consider the phrase 'he would hold himself to account for a moment spent in other than obedience.' This shows intense mindfulness.
This is a high level of spiritual vigilance.
4
Observe his courage and detachment
Notice how he spoke the truth fearlessly and attacked innovators, while turning away from worldly positions and rulers.
Detachment is not passivity; it includes active defense of truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان رحمه الله حسن السمت مواظبا على الجماعة والتلاوة يختم كل جمعة ختمة، ويختم فى رمضان كل يوم، ويعتكف في المنارة الشرقية وكان كثير النوافل والأذكار ويحيى ليلة العيدين بالصلاة والذكر وكان يحاسب نفسه على لحظة تذهب في غير طاعة يصدع بالحق لايخاف في الله لومة لائم، ويسطو على أعداء الله المبتدعة، وكـان مـعـرضـا عن الدنيا والمناصب بعد عرضها عليه كثيرا، قليل الالتفات إلى الأمراء وأصحاب الدينا.
And he, may Allah have mercy on him, was of good appearance, regular in congregational prayer and recitation of the Qur'an. He would complete a full recitation (khatma) every Friday, and every day in Ramadan, and he would perform i'tikaf in the Eastern Minaret. He was abundant in supererogatory prayers and remembrances (adhkar). He would revive the night of the two 'Ids with prayer and remembrance. He would hold himself to account for a moment spent in other than obedience. He would speak out the truth, not fearing the blame of any blamer for the sake of Allah. He would assault the enemies of Allah, the innovators. He was turning away from the world and positions after they were frequently offered to him, paying little attention to princes and worldly people.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage describes Ibn Asakir's personal piety and asceticism. He had a good demeanor (حسن السمت). He was regular in congregational prayer and Qur'an recitation. He would complete a full khatm of the Qur'an each Friday and every day in Ramadan. He performed i'tikaf in the Eastern Minaret (probably the minaret of the Umayyad Mosque). He was abundant in supererogatory prayers (nawafil) and dhikr. He would revive the nights of the two 'Ids with prayer and dhikr. He held himself accountable for even a moment wasted. He was outspoken for truth, fearless of blame. He would assault (i.e., refute) heretics and innovators. He was detached from worldly positions and money, often offered to him, and paid little attention to rulers and wealthy people.

Misconception: Some may think 'يصدع بالحق' means that Ibn Asakir caused division or discord by speaking the truth.
Correction: The phrase actually means he openly proclaimed the truth without fear, not that he caused division.

Ibn Asakir's Poetry on Trust
Ibn Asakir's concluding poem on trust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir used poetry to teach the importance of keeping secrets in gatherings, reflecting Islamic trustworthiness.

Core Idea
Poetry as a reminder: secrets shared in gatherings are trusts.

This passage offers a glimpse into Ibn Asakir's practice of concluding his teaching sessions with poetry. The poem he recited emphasizes a crucial moral lesson: what is shared in confidence must remain a trust. Let's explore this verse and its lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the rhythm of the poetry and the direct moral message.
The poem uses a metaphor of a house deposit for a secret.
2
Understand the context
Ibn Asakir, known as al-Hafiz, often ended his teaching sessions (majlis) with a poem. This shows his integration of knowledge and moral admonition.
3
Analyze the poem's message
The poem criticizes someone who, entrusted with a secret, reveals it willingly. It declares this a grave sin and reminds that the Prophet said gatherings are covered with trusts.
The word 'al-Mukhtar' refers to the Prophet Muhammad.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this teaching applies to daily life: secrets confided in you are trusts that must be kept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طرف من شعره : للحافظ شعر كثير قلما أملى مجلسا إلا وختمه بشيء منه قال السمعانى وأنشدنی لنفسه ببغداد : وصاحب خان ما استودعته وأنى ما لا يليق بأرباب الديانات وأظهر السر مختارا بلا سبب وذاك والله من أوفى الجنايات أما أتاه عن المختار في خبر ان المجالس تغشى بالأمانات
A sample of his poetry: Al-Hafiz had much poetry; rarely did he dictate a session (majlis) except that he concluded it with something of it. Al-Sam'ani said: And he recited to me his own composition in Baghdad: 'And the owner of a house in which I deposited something, how can that be appropriate for religious people? And he revealed the secret, of his own accord, without reason. That, by Allah, is among the gravest sins. Has it not reached him from al-Mukhtar (the Prophet) in a report that gatherings are covered with trusts?'
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage gives a glimpse of Ibn Asakir's poetic output. His sessions often ended with a verse. The quoted poem criticizes a person who, entrusted with a secret (deposited in a house), revealed it voluntarily. The poet declares that this is a grave sin, and reminds that the Prophet (al-Mukhtar) said that gatherings are covered with trusts (i.e., secrets should be kept). This reflects the importance of trustworthiness in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem is about a literal house owner and a deposit of money.
Correction: The 'owner of a house' is a metaphor for someone entrusted with a secret, not literal house ownership.

?
The poem declares that revealing a secret without reason is a grave sin. Reflect on your own habits: do you guard the secrets of others as you would guard your own? What is one change you will make today to become more trustworthy?

Fakhr al-Din Ibn 'Asakir: Acclaimed by Contemporaries
Fakhr al-Din's education in hadith and the acclaim he received in Baghdad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the high scholarly status of Fakhr al-Din ibn 'Asakir as attested by his contemporaries in Baghdad and by later scholars.

Core Idea
Fakhr al-Din was hailed as unmatched among the scholars of his era.

This passage introduces us to Fakhr al-Din ibn 'Asakir, a prominent Shafi'i scholar and hadith specialist. The Baghdadis who saw him declared they had never seen his like, and later scholars ranked him as the pride of the Shafi'is and the imam of the hadith folk. Let's examine the firsthand testimonies and accolades that immortalize his reputation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on his arrival and praise
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the Baghdadis compared him to other notable figures.
The comparison shows his perceived superiority.
2
Understand the comparison with other greats
The story from al-Qasim's father shows that Fakhr al-Din was considered superior even to the vizier and Ibn al-Sam'ani.

This shows the family's scholarly legacy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحديث النورية فدرس بها إلى حين وفاته ولما قدم إلى بغداد أعجب به البغداديون وقالوا قدم علينا من دمشق ثلاثة ما رأينا مثلهم : الشيخ يوسف الدمشقى والصائن أبو الحسين هبة الله بن الحسن وأخوه أبو القسم، وقال القاسم حدثني أبي رحمه الله قال كنت يوما اقرأ على شيخنا أبي الفتح المختار بن عبد الحميد وهو يتحدث مع جماعة بالعجمية فقال قدم علينا الوزير أبو على فقلنا ما رأينا مثله ثم قدم علينا أبو سعد بن السمعاني فقلنا ما رأينا مثله حتى قدم علينا هذا فلم نر مثله. وقال ابن قاضي شهبة : فخر الشافعية وإمام أهل الحديث في زمانهم وحامل لوائهم .
وقال أبو شامة في ذيل الروضتين عند ترجمة الفخر بن عساكر وهذا البيت (بنو عساكر بيت جليل من الدمشقيين كثير الفضلاء والحفاظ والأمناء جمع هذا البيت رياسة الدين والدنيا، وأجلهم في زمانه دينا وعلما هذا فخر الدين ابن عساكر وفي القرن الذى قبله عماه الصائن هبة الله والحافظ أبو القاسم .
وكان رحمه الله حسن السمت مواظبا على الجماعة والتلاوة يختم كل جمعة ختمة، ويختم فى رمضان كل يوم، ويعتكف في المنارة الشرقية وكان كثير النوافل والأذكار ويحيى ليلة العيدين بالصلاة والذكر وكان يحاسب نفسه على لحظة تذهب في غير طاعة يصدع بالحق لايخاف في الله لومة لائم، ويسطو على أعداء الله المبتدعة، وكـان مـعـرضـا عن الدنيا والمناصب بعد عرضها عليه كثيرا، قليل الالتفات إلى الأمراء وأصحاب الدينا.
He studied the hadith of al-Nuriyya and taught it until his death. When he came to Baghdad, the Baghdadis admired him and said: 'Three came to us from Damascus, we have not seen their like: Shaykh Yusuf al-Dimashqi, al-Sa'in Abu al-Husayn Hibat Allah ibn al-Hasan, and his brother Abu al-Qasim.' Al-Qasim said: My father narrated to me, may Allah have mercy on him, said: I was one day reading to our shaykh Abu al-Fath al-Mukhtar ibn 'Abd al-Hamid while he was speaking with a group in non-Arabic, and he said: 'The vizier Abu 'Ali came to us and we said we had not seen his like. Then Abu Sa'd ibn al-Sam'ani came to us and we said we had not seen his like. Then this one came to us and we have not seen his like.' Ibn Qadi Shuhba said: 'The pride of the Shafi'is and the imam of the people of hadith in their time and the carrier of their banner.' Abu Shama said in his supplement to al-Rawdatayn in the biography of Fakhr ibn 'Asakir: 'This family (Banu 'Asakir) is a noble house of the Damascenes, many virtuous ones, hadith memorizers, and trustworthy ones. This house combines leadership in religion and world. The most eminent of them in his time in religion and knowledge is this Fakhr al-Din ibn 'Asakir, and in the preceding century his paternal uncle al-Sa'in Hibat Allah and the hafiz Abu al-Qasim.' He was, may Allah have mercy on him, of good demeanor, assiduous in attending congregational prayer and recitation, completing a complete recitation of the Qur'an every Friday, and completing one every day in Ramadan, and he would seclude himself in the eastern minaret. He was abundant in supererogatory prayers and remembrances, and would revive the nights of the two Eids with prayer and remembrance. He would hold himself accountable for any moment spent in other than obedience. He would speak the truth fearlessly, not fearing the blame of any blamer for Allah's sake, and he would attack the enemies of Allah, the innovators. He was heedless of worldly life and positions after they were often offered to him, giving little attention to princes and people of worldly possessions.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Fakhr al-Din ibn 'Asakir. It begins by mentioning his study of a hadith collection and his teaching of it. Then it recounts his arrival in Baghdad and the admiration of the Baghdadis, who compared him favorably to other notable figures who had visited them. Several scholars praise him: Ibn Qadi Shuhba calls him the pride of the Shafi'is and the imam of hadith scholars; Abu Shama describes the nobility of the Banu 'Asakir family and singles him out as the most eminent in his time. The passage then details his personal character: his devotion to congregational prayer, Qur'an recitation, fasting, seclusion, and supererogatory acts of worship. He was scrupulous in accounting for his time, spoke truth fearlessly, and combated innovators. He was detached from worldly positions and wealth, paying little attention to rulers and the wealthy.

الحافظ
أهل الحديث

Fakhr al-Din's Piety and Devotion
Details of Fakhr al-Din's worship, asceticism, and moral character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the personal piety, asceticism, and courage of Fakhr al-Din ibn 'Asakir as described in his biography.

Core Idea
His worship was constant, his truthfulness fearless, and his heart detached from worldly allure.

After establishing his scholarly eminence, the biography turns to Fakhr al-Din's personal character. He was not only a great scholar but also a devout worshipper, a vigilant ascetic, and a bold defender of orthodoxy against innovators. Let's explore the description of his spiritual practices and moral integrity.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of his worship
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the specific practices: congregational prayer, Qur'an recitation, fasting, i'tikaf.
He would complete a full Qur'an recitation every Friday and every day in Ramadan.
2
Note his self-accountability
The text says he held himself accountable for every moment spent in other than obedience.
This shows his spiritual vigilance.
3
Observe his courage and opposition to innovators
He spoke the truth without fear and attacked the enemies of Allah, the innovators (ahl al-bid'a).
This highlights his role as a defender of orthodoxy.
4
Reflect on his detachment from worldly wealth
He was disinterested in worldly positions and wealth, offering little attention to princes and the wealthy.

وقال أبو شامة في ذيل الروضتين عند ترجمة الفخر بن عساكر وهذا البيت (بنو عساكر بيت جليل من الدمشقيين كثير الفضلاء والحفاظ والأمناء جمع هذا البيت رياسة الدين والدنيا، وأجلهم في زمانه دينا وعلما هذا فخر الدين ابن عساكر وفي القرن الذى قبله عماه الصائن هبة الله والحافظ أبو القاسم .

He studied the hadith of al-Nuriyya and taught it until his death. When he came to Baghdad, the Baghdadis admired him and said: 'Three came to us from Damascus, we have not seen their like: Shaykh Yusuf al-Dimashqi, al-Sa'in Abu al-Husayn Hibat Allah ibn al-Hasan, and his brother Abu al-Qasim.' Al-Qasim said: My father narrated to me, may Allah have mercy on him, said: I was one day reading to our shaykh Abu al-Fath al-Mukhtar ibn 'Abd al-Hamid while he was speaking with a group in non-Arabic, and he said: 'The vizier Abu 'Ali came to us and we said we had not seen his like. Then Abu Sa'd ibn al-Sam'ani came to us and we said we had not seen his like. Then this one came to us and we have not seen his like.' Ibn Qadi Shuhba said: 'The pride of the Shafi'is and the imam of the people of hadith in their time and the carrier of their banner.' Abu Shama said in his supplement to al-Rawdatayn in the biography of Fakhr ibn 'Asakir: 'This family (Banu 'Asakir) is a noble house of the Damascenes, many virtuous ones, hadith memorizers, and trustworthy ones. This house combines leadership in religion and world. The most eminent of them in his time in religion and knowledge is this Fakhr al-Din ibn 'Asakir, and in the preceding century his paternal uncle al-Sa'in Hibat Allah and the hafiz Abu al-Qasim.' He was, may Allah have mercy on him, of good demeanor, assiduous in attending congregational prayer and recitation, completing a complete recitation of the Qur'an every Friday, and completing one every day in Ramadan, and he would seclude himself in the eastern minaret. He was abundant in supererogatory prayers and remembrances, and would revive the nights of the two Eids with prayer and remembrance. He would hold himself accountable for any moment spent in other than obedience. He would speak the truth fearlessly, not fearing the blame of any blamer for Allah's sake, and he would attack the enemies of Allah, the innovators. He was heedless of worldly life and positions after they were often offered to him, giving little attention to princes and people of worldly possessions.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Fakhr al-Din ibn 'Asakir. It begins by mentioning his study of a hadith collection and his teaching of it. Then it recounts his arrival in Baghdad and the admiration of the Baghdadis, who compared him favorably to other notable figures who had visited them. Several scholars praise him: Ibn Qadi Shuhba calls him the pride of the Shafi'is and the imam of hadith scholars; Abu Shama describes the nobility of the Banu 'Asakir family and singles him out as the most eminent in his time. The passage then details his personal character: his devotion to congregational prayer, Qur'an recitation, fasting, seclusion, and supererogatory acts of worship. He was scrupulous in accounting for his time, spoke truth fearlessly, and combated innovators. He was detached from worldly positions and wealth, paying little attention to rulers and the wealthy.

Ibn Asakir's Works and Death Details
Listing Ibn Asakir's completed and unfinished works, then his death and burial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the literary output and historical circumstances surrounding the death of Ibn Asakir, including the challenges in locating his grave.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir's unfinished works and elusive grave remind us of the fragility of historical memory.

In this passage, we learn not only about the vast scholarly output of Ibn Asakir but also the details of his passing and burial. The list of works, including the massive unfinished al-Abdal, shows his ambition. The death notice records the involvement of Sultan Salah al-Din, and the editorial lament about the unknown grave connects us to the ongoing work of historical preservation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the biographical details sink in before interpreting.
2
Identify the two main sections
The passage has two clear parts: a list of works (completed and uncompleted) and a death/burial notice with an editorial note. Notice how they are distinct but together form a complete biographical sketch.
3
Focus on the list of works
Identify which works were completed and which were not. Note the enormous scale of al-Abdal (200 parts if completed). The list includes works like Dhamm al-Rafidah and al-Sifat, and several Fada'il works.
The total is forty works.
4
Examine the death and editorial note
Observe the date (11 Rajab 571 AH), the funeral prayer led by Qutb al-Din al-Naysaburi, and attendance by Sultan Salah al-Din. Then read the editor's note: the grave is lost, only a probable location. Reflect on why this matters for historical preservation.
The editorial note is not from Ibn Asakir's original text but from a later editor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ماتم من مصنفاته ومما لم يتم : ( الأبدال ) ولو تم كان مقداره ۲۰۰ جزء أو أكثر و ( ذم الرافضة ) ( الصفات ) ( فضل بيت المقدس) (فضل الجهاد ) ( فضل قريش وأهل البيت والأنصار والأشعرين ) ( فضل المدينة ) ( فضل مكة ) ( مسند مكحول وأبى حنيفة وأشياء غير ذلك تبلغ أربعين مصنفا .

وفاته ومدفنه : توفى فى حادى عشر رجب سنة احدى وسبعين وخمسمائة بدمشق وصلى عليه الشيخ قطب الدين النيسابورى، وحضر جنازته بالميدان والصلاة عليه السلطان صلاح الدين بن أيوب، ودفن عند والده وأهله بمقابر باب الصغير شرقی قبر معاوية رحمهم الله تعالى(۱) .

من بواعث الأسف اننا لم نجتمع بمن يعرف قبره اليوم، وقد بحثنا كثيرا لتحقيق موضعه فلم نتجاوز حد الترجيح في قبر واقع في الشرق القبلي لمدفن معاوية كتب عليه بقلم كوفى طامس ما يشبه اسم الحافظ وسنة وفاته، ولعلنا نسترشد الاخصائيين بمعرفة الخطوط القديمة حتى إذا توكدنا أمره جددنا معالمه اذ لاحياة لأمة نسيت ماضيها .
This is what was completed of his works and what was not completed: (al-Abdal) and if it had been completed its size would have been 200 parts or more, and (Blame of the Rafidah), (The Attributes), (The Virtue of Jerusalem), (The Virtue of Jihad), (The Virtue of Quraysh, the People of the House, the Ansar, and the Ash'aris), (The Virtue of Medina), (The Virtue of Mecca), (Musnad of Makhul and Abu Hanifa and other things that amount to forty works).

His death and his grave: He died on the eleventh of Rajab of the year 571 in Damascus, and the prayer over him was led by Shaykh Qutb al-Din al-Naysaburi, and his funeral procession was attended at the Maydan and the prayer over him was attended by Sultan Salah al-Din ibn Ayyub, and he was buried next to his father and family in the cemetery of Bab al-Saghir, east of the grave of Mu'awiya, may God have mercy on them all.

It is a cause of regret that we have not met anyone who knows his grave today, and we have researched much to verify its location but we did not go beyond the limit of probability concerning a grave located in the southeastern area of the tomb of Mu'awiya on which is written in a faded Kufic script something resembling the name of the Hafiz and his year of death. Perhaps we will seek guidance from specialists in ancient scripts so that if we confirm his matter we will renew his landmarks, for there is no life for a nation that forgets its past.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a biographical conclusion that first lists Ibn Asakir's works—both completed and unfinished—then records his death and burial, and finally includes an editor's note about the current unknown location of his grave. The list of works includes several titles; notably, the unfinished work 'al-Abdal' alone would have filled 200 volumes if completed. Other uncompleted works are listed, such as 'Dhamm al-Rafidah' (Blame of the Rafidah), 'al-Sifat' (The Attributes), and several 'Fada'il' (Virtues) books, along with a Musnad of Makhul and Abu Hanifa, all totaling forty works. The death notice states he died on 11 Rajab 571 AH in Damascus, with the funeral prayer led by Qutb al-Din al-Naysaburi and attended by Sultan Salah al-Din. He was buried at Bab al-Saghir, east of Mu'awiya's grave. The editor's note expresses regret that the exact grave is now unknown, despite research, and hopes that experts in old scripts can help identify and restore it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The editorial note about the grave is part of Ibn Asakir's original text.
Correction: The editorial note is a modern addition by the editor of the book, not from Ibn Asakir himself.

Death and Burial of Imam Ibn Asakir
Biographical note on death and burial with editorial reflection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical details of Imam Ibn Asakir's death and burial, and appreciate the importance of preserving historical markers.

Core Idea
A nation that forgets its past has no life.

This lesson presents a brief biographical note on the passing of Imam Ibn Asakir, one of the great hadith scholars of Damascus. It records the date of his death, the funeral attended by Sultan Salah al-Din, and his burial place. An editorial footnote adds a poignant reflection on the loss of his grave and the duty to remember our heritage.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical note
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It begins with the date of death, then describes the funeral and burial. Pay attention to the names and locations mentioned.
The passage includes both the original text and an editorial footnote.
2
Identify key figures and places
Note the names: Shaykh Qutb al-Din al-Naysaburi (who led the prayer), Sultan Salah al-Din ibn Ayyub (who attended), and the cemetery Bab al-Saghir, east of Mu'awiya's grave. These details connect to Damascene history.
3
Reflect on the editorial footnote
The footnote expresses regret that the exact grave location is lost. It describes efforts to identify it and emphasizes the importance of preserving historical markers. Ask yourself: Why is this footnote included?
This reflects a broader lesson about the value of history and heritage.
